SECTION 6.0
CURRENT INVESTIGATIONS

6-1

6.0

CURRENT INVESTIGATIONS

Archaeological resource surveys were conducted for all the proposed tower sites, as well as their
associated access and utility corridors. Twenty-eight new archaeological sites were recorded
during the archaeological surveys. In addition to the newly recorded archaeological sites, two
previously recorded archaeological sites were revisited and updated. Historic resource surveys
were conducted for all the proposed tower sites where there was a potential for effects on
historic-age resources within the visual APE of the proposed tower. The results of these
investigations are presented below and organized by AOR and tower site.
6.1

RIO GRANDE STATION AREA OF RESPONSIBILITY

A total of 31 proposed tower sites along with their associated utility and access corridors were
surveyed for archaeological and historic resources within the Rio Grande Station AOR. An
additional four relocated tower sites were surveyed for RGC Inside Mustang Gate Chapeno
Alternate, RGC Speedios Escobares Tertiary Option 1,RGC Near Blas Chapas La Puerta
Primary, and RGC North of Silvertanks Military Road Primary tower sites. In addition, several
realigned access roads were also subject to survey after the original access roads were surveyed.
A total of 25 archaeological sites were recorded during the surveys conducted within the Rio
Grande City Station AOR. Another two previously recorded archaeological sites were revisited
and updated. Summaries of the results of the archaeological and historical resource surveys are
outlined in the following sections by tower.
6.1.1 RGC Inside Mustang Gate Chapeno Alternate
6.1.1.1 Archaeological Resource Investigations
The RGC Inside Mustang Gate Chapeno Alternate original tower site was located within a
heavily disturbed area that was used as a gas or oil well (see Photographs B-1 to B-8). Unused
pipes were located across the tower site and the pad itself was elevated with approximately 4 feet
of fill. In addition, the access road and access and utility corridor were both already located on
an existing maintained road which encompassed both the 30-foot access corridor and 60-foot
access and utility corridor. Surface visibility along the access and utility corridors and the tower
pad was excellent (~90 percent). Given the existing disturbance at the original tower site, the
overall good ground visibility, and low potential for subsurface deposits the RGC Inside
Mustang Gate Chapeno Alternate tower site and associated access and utility corridors were
subject to intensive pedestrian survey and no supplemental shovel testing was conducted.
After the initial survey of the RGC Inside Mustang Gate Chapeno Alternate tower site location,
the tower site was moved to the east in order to avoid potential hazardous material associated
with the original tower site. The RGC Inside Mustang Gate Chapeno Alternate (relocated) tower
site was also subjected to an intensive pedestrian survey (see Photographs B-9 to B-16). In
addition, the pedestrian survey was supplemented with the excavation of three STPs (T1-ST1 to
T1-ST3) across the tower site (Table 6-1; see Figure A-44). No archaeological resources were
found in either the original or relocated tower sites and no additional archaeological
investigations are recommended for this tower site or its associated access and utility corridors.

Cultural Resources Inventory and Impacts Report, Rio Grande City, McAllen, and
Weslaco Stations’ Areas of Responsibility Remote Video Surveillance Upgrade Project

6.1.1.2 Historic Resource Investigations
The previously recorded Chapeno Cemetery (Cemetery ID No. SR-C053) is not located within
the visual APE, but is located on the southeast side of the access road (Chapeno Road),
approximately 0.85 km (0.53 mile) southwest from the center of the proposed tower site. No
established date is known. It is approximately 1.15 acres and is surrounded by a 3-foot wire
fence with wood post supports. A metal gate sits at the west (primary) side of the cemetery. The
tower site is visible from all points in the cemetery but does not constitute as an adverse visual
element, however, it is recommended that the utility corridor be placed on the opposite side of
the access road to avoid disturbing the cemetery. Additional structures present in the visual APE
consist of small residential buildings and utility structures.
Cemetery Chapeno-2
One newly recorded aboveground/architectural historic resource, a cemetery (Chapeno-2), was
recorded during the survey of the proposed RGC Inside Mustang Gate Chapeno Alternate tower
site and its associated access and utility corridors (see Figure A-45). The unnamed cemetery is
0.13 km (0.8 mile) southwest from the center of the proposed tower site on the southeast side of
the access road. Cemetery Chapeno-1 measures approximately 18 feet north to south by 14 feet
east to west and has an estimated area of 261 square feet (see Photograph B-17). The cemetery
is bounded by a low (3-foot-high) rock wall made from local stone and clay grout.
Three headstones were recorded within the cemetery. Headstone 1 (HS-1), the northernmost
headstone, is made of concrete and is broken apart and incomplete (see Photograph B-18). A
fragment from the headstone had a crucifix and dove motif embossed on its surface. Given the
orientation of HS-1, the headstone marks an east-to-west oriented grave. Headstone 2 (HS-19) is
an intact headstone, crucifix in form and made of rock and concrete (see Photograph B-20).
Given the orientation of HS-2 the headstone marks an east-to-west oriented grave. The third
headstone (HS-3) is the southernmost headstone and is made of concrete. HS-3 is partially
broken apart but most likely is crucifix in form (see Photograph B-21). Given the orientation of
HS-3 it marks a north-to-south oriented grave.
The Chapeno-2 cemetery is not mapped in the Texas Archeological Sites Atlas and no additional
information is known about the cemetery. The proposed tower site is visible from the cemetery,
but is not considered an adverse visual element due to the presence of existing towers. It is
recommended that the access road be moved or the utility corridor be placed on the opposite side
of the access road to avoid disturbing the cemetery in any way.

Weslaco Stations’ Areas of Responsibility Remote Video Surveillance Upgrade Project

6-3

6.1.2 RGC North of Bench Landing Salineno Primary
6.1.2.1 Archaeological Resource Investigations
The archaeological survey of the RGC North of Bench landing Salineno Primary Tower site and
its associated access and utility corridor consisted of an intensive pedestrian survey
supplemented with the excavation of three STPs at the proposed tower site (T1-STP1 to T2STP3) and the excavation of four STPs along the proposed access and utility corridor (T2-STP1
to T2-STP4) (see Photographs B-21 to B-28). Modern material associated with dumping from a
residential area to the east of the survey corridor was noted in the two initial STPs excavated
along the access and utility corridor. All of the material was determined to be modern in the
field and discarded. As a result, none of the STPs were positive for historic or prehistoric
cultural material (Table 6-2; see Figure A-46). A surface scatter of prehistoric artifacts was
noted across the proposed RGC North of Bench Landing Salineno Primary tower site and was
recorded as archaeological site 41SR444.

41SR444 (Salineno-P-1)
The site was originally recorded from a surface scatter of artifacts. Three transect STPs (T11STP1 to T1-STP33) and three delineation STPs (D-1 to D-3) were excavated across the area of
Cultural Resources Inventory and Impacts Report, Rio Grande City, McAllen, and

6-4

the surface scatter but none of the STPs were positive for cultural material (Table 6-3; see Figure
A-47). A typical shovel test profile for a STP excavated within the site boundary consisted of a
light yellowish brown (10YR 6/4) sandy clay to a depth of 50 cmbgs. Given the excellent
ground visibility and lack of subsurface deposits, the site was defined by the surface expression
of artifacts. Site delineation was confined largely to the proposed tower location survey
boundaries, but a limited surface reconnaissance was conducted along the landform extending to
the north and northwest. Additional cultural material was noted to the north and northwest and
the site extends beyond the survey limits to the north. As a result, the site boundaries, as
currently defined, are considered incomplete. The portion of the site recorded measures 55
meters by 58 meters and has a total area of 1,567 square meters. The site is positioned on a low
terrace that overlooks a junction of two unnamed arroyos to the south (see Photograph B-29).
Vegetation at the site was dominated by an understory of buffelgrass (Cenchrus ciliaris) and an
overstory of honey mesquite (Prosopis glandulosa) and huisache (Acacia farnesiana).

Artifacts recorded from the surface during the delineation of the site were dominated by lithic
debitage (30 primary flakes, 59 secondary flakes, 164 tertiary flakes, and 23 pieces of angular
debris) of chert, basalt, rhyolite, and chalcedony. In addition to lithic debitage, several chert
stone tools were recorded from the surface, including one unhafted biface fragment (T.1
Photographs B-30 and B-31), two multidirectional core tools (T.2 and T.3; see Photographs B-32
to B-35), and one unifacial flake tool (T.4). None of the artifacts recorded from the surface were
diagnostic of a particular temporal period or culture. One feature was noted during the
delineation of the site, which consisted of a thermally altered rock concentration of 25 fragments
within and area measuring 50 centimeters north-south by 65 centimeters east-west (see
Photograph B-36). In addition to the material comprising the feature, nine fragments of
thermally altered rock were noted across the site area, which suggests that other thermal features
may be present. Given the artifact assemblage and feature, the site probably represents a
repeated-use, open campsite. The site may have the potential to address research questions
regarding prehistoric adaptations in south Texas including stone tool manufacture, resource
processing, and subsistence. The current archaeological investigations were limited to the survey
area of the tower site and additional portions of the site extend outside the current survey
boundary to the north and northwest. As a result, the site has the potential to provide
information on prehistoric adaptations in the area and the eligibility of the site for the NRHP is
unknown.

6.1.2.2 Historic Resource Investigations
One previously recorded aboveground/architectural historic resource was examined within the
visual APE of the proposed RGC N of Bench Landing Salineno Primary tower site. The
Salineno Cemetery (Cemetery ID No. SR-C057) (see Photograph B-37) is located 0.49 km (0.31
mile) north from the center of the tower site. No established date is known. It is approximately
27 acres and is surrounded by a 3-foot-tall chain-link fence. A chainlink gate with red brick
posts and a cast-iron sign that reads CEMETERIO CATOLICO DE SALINENO TEXAS sits on
the west (primary) side of the cemetery. A flagpole sits to the left of the gate. The tower site is
likely to be visible all points in the cemetery, but does not constitute as an adverse visual effect.
Additional structures present in the visual APE consist of small residential and commercial
buildings.

The site was originally recorded from a surface scatter of artifacts found within the footprint of
an unimproved dirt road. Surface visibility within the road was good to excellent (90 to 100
percent), but quickly dropped to poor (0 to 10 percent) outside of the dirt road footprint. An

The site is positioned on an upland flat that slopes very gentle to the south and east to a tributary
of the Arroyo de los Mudos. Vegetation at the site is dominated by an overstory of honey
mesquite and an understory of buffelgrass (see Photograph B-53).
All the artifacts recorded at the site were recorded from the disturbed roadbed. Artifacts
recorded at the site were dominated by lithic debitage (two primary flakes, four secondary flakes,
14 tertiary flakes, and five shatter) of chert. A single stone tool (T.1), a hafted biface, was also
recorded at the site (see Photograph B-54 and B-55). The hafted biface could not be identified to
a known type and may represent a preform. Given the limited artifact assemblage, the site
probably represents a single-occupation, open campsite where very limited resource processing
may have taken place. Given the site's limited artifact assemblage, the site has little or no
potential to answer questions regarding prehistoric settlement, subsistence, and resource
processing in south Texas.
IO-BlueandWhite-P-1
The IO (IO-BlueandWhite-P-1) was originally recorded from a sparse surface scatter of lithic
material noted within the existing road (see Photograph B-56). The surface artifact assemblage
consisted of a distal biface fragment (see Photograph B-57 and B-58) and two tertiary flakes of
chert. An additional five delineation STPs (D-1 to D-5) were excavated around the surface
scatter but no additional artifacts were recovered (Table 6-7; see Figure A-51). Given the limited
number of artifacts that were found in a disturbed context, the locus is considered to be an IO
and is recommended not eligible for the NRHP.

material (Table 6-8; see Figure A-52). Four surface scatters of artifacts were noted during the
survey that resulted in the recording of four archaeological sites, 41SR434, 41SR435, 41SR436
and 41SR437.

41SR434 (BlueandWhite-A-1)
Site 41SR434 was originally located from a surface scatter noted at transect STP 2-1 along the
access/utility corridor for the proposed tower site. An additional six delineation STPs were
excavated at the site, none of which were positive for cultural material (Table 6-9). The soil
profile consisted of a single stratum of pale brown (10YR 6/3) silt loam that extended to a depth
of 50 centimeters below ground surface before encountering a shale-like bedrock. Surface
visibility at the site was good to excellent (70 to 80 percent). Given the good surface visibility
and lack of any subsurface artifacts, the site boundaries were determined by the surface
expression of artifacts. Site delineation was limited to the west by a fence and property line and
to the east by the limits of the survey corridor. A reconnaissance of the edge of the survey area
noted that additional artifacts extended beyond the survey corridor to the east. As a result, the
site boundary is considered incomplete and the site extends to the east. The portion of the site
recorded within the survey corridor measures 40 meters north-south by 30 meters east-west and
has a total site area of 750 square meters (see Figure A-53).

The site is positioned on a slight rise of a terrace that overlooks a tributary of Arroyo la Minita to
the east and north (Photograph B-64). Vegetation at the site was typical of thorn scrub brush
lands and is dominated by blackbrush acacia (Acacia rigidula) and lotebush (Ziziphus
obtusifolia). Other vegetation noted near the site includes guayacan (Guaiacum angustifolium),
bee brush (Aloysia gratissima), and Texas ranger (Leucophyllum frutescens). An understory of
buffelgrass is noted across the site becoming dense adjacent to the existing road. The landform
rises slightly to the east of the access road before dropping down into the arroyo. A tributary for
Arroyo La Minita lies 50 meters to the east and the main channel of the Arroyo lies 200 meters
to the north.
Cultural Resources Inventory and Impacts Report, Rio Grande City, McAllen, and
Weslaco Stations’ Areas of Responsibility Remote Video Surveillance Upgrade Project

Final
November 2016

6-10

No subsurface artifacts were noted in any of the seven STPs that were excavated across the site.
Artifacts recorded from the surface included lithic debitage (four primary, 13 secondary, 10
tertiary, and six angular debris) and stone tools (one hafted biface [Early Triangular] [T.1], one
unhafted biface [T.2], one unhafted unifacial flake tool, and two core tools [1 possible end
scraper T.3]) (see Photographs B-65 to B-70). A possible deflated thermal feature, composed of
12 thermally altered rocks scattered across a 3-meter-diameter area, was recorded at the site just
outside of the survey corridor (see Photograph B-71). The presence of an Early Triangular
hafted biface would tentatively date the occupation of the site to the Early Archaic period. Given
the artifact assemblage and the one feature recorded, the site probably represents an Early
Archaic open campsite. Artifacts were more heavily concentrated near the eastern boundary of
the survey corridor and were noted extending beyond the survey corridor to the east. Given the
presence of stone tools, diagnostic artifacts, and a potential thermal feature, the site is considered
to have good potential to answer questions regarding prehistoric settlement and adaptation in the
area, including possible subsistence information. The site is recommended for additional
archaeological testing and is recommended to have an undetermined eligibility for the NRHP
until additional testing is conducted to determine its eligibility for the NRHP.
41SR435 (BlueandWhite-A-2)
Site 41SR435 was originally recorded from the observation of surface material near a transect
STP (2-3). Six additional delineation STPs (D-1 to D-6) were excavated across the site, one of
which was positive for subsurface cultural material (Table 6-10; see Figure A-54). As a result,
the site was delineated predominantly by the surface expression of artifacts. A typical shovel test
profile from a delineation STP (D-3) excavated within the site boundary consisted of a pale
brown (10YR 6/3) clay to a depth of 45 centimeters below ground surface before encountering
bedrock shale. Site delineation was limited to the west by a fence and property line, and to the
east by the survey boundary, which was set 60 feet east of the centerline of the existing
unimproved road. A reconnaissance of the eastern edge of the survey corridor noted additional
artifacts extending beyond the survey corridor to the east and the site boundaries are not
considered complete. The site, as currently defined, measures 69 meters north to south by 40
meters east to west and has a total site area of 1,812 square meters.

current survey area. Vegetation noted at the site was consistent with vegetation typical of thorn

within the recorded portion of the site, the site probably represents a short-term, singleoccupation open campsite of unknown cultural affiliation. Given the moderate artifact density
and presence of stone tools, the site has the potential to provide information on prehistoric
settlement and resource procurement in south Texas. Because of the limitation of the survey
corridor, only a portion of the site was delineated and additional archaeological investigations
would be needed to determine the site's eligibility for the NRHP.
41SR436 (BlueandWhite-A-3)
Site 41SR436 was originally recorded from the observation of surface material within the road
where it shifts from the west side of a fence line to the east side of a fence line. One transect
STP excavated within the boundaries of the site was negative for subsurface cultural material.
An additional five delineation STPs (D-1 to D-4) were excavated across the site, but none were
positive for cultural material (Table 6-11; see Figure A-55). A typical soil profile from a
delineation STP (D-2) excavated within the site boundary consisted of an upper stratum of light
gray (10YR 7/2) dry clay to a depth of 20 centimeters below ground surface followed by a
brownish yellow (10YR 6/6) clay from 20 to 50 centimeters below ground surface. Given the
lack of subsurface cultural material, the site was delineated by the surface expression of artifacts.
Site delineation was limited to the west and east by a fence and property lines and the survey
corridor boundaries, which were set 60 feet east and west of the centerline of the existing
unimproved road. A reconnaissance of the eastern edge of the survey corridor noted additional
artifacts extending beyond the survey corridor to the east and the site boundaries are not
considered complete. The site, as currently defined, measures 129 meters north to south by 30
meters east to west and has a total site area of 1,854 square meters.

The site is on a low terrace situated east of a confluence of Arroyo la Minita and one of its minor
tributaries (see Photograph B-73). The site occupies a relatively flat area that rises very
gradually to the east and north beyond the current survey corridor. Vegetation noted at the site
was consistent with vegetation typical of thorn scrub brush lands.
All artifacts recorded at the site were from the surface. No subsurface artifacts were noted in the
transect or delineation STPs excavated at the site. Artifacts recovered from the site were limited
to lithic debitage (four primary, seven secondary, 13 tertiary, two angular debris, and two tested
cobbles) of chert. No stone tools, diagnostic material, or features were recorded at the site.
Additional artifacts were noted outside of the survey corridor continuing to the east. Given the
limited artifact assemblage within the recorded portion of the site, the site probably represents a
short-term, single-occupation open campsite. Given the light artifact assemblage, the portion of
the site within the survey corridor has little or no potential to provide significant information on
the prehistoric settlement and adaptations in south Texas. While the recorded portion of the site
is limited, only a portion of the site was within the survey corridor and recorded. As a result,
additional work would be needed to determine the eligibility of the site for listing in the NRHP.
41SR437 (BlueandWhite-A-4)
Site 41SR437 is located on a broad, very gently sloping terrace that overlooks the main channel
of Arroyo la Minita to the west. The site is located on both sides of the road. Five additional
STPs (D-1 to D-5) were excavated during the delineation of the site (Table 6-12; see Figure A56). Transect and delineation STPs excavated within the site area failed to record any subsurface
cultural material. In addition, the disturbed area of the road widens within the site, taking up 40
feet of the 60-foot access/utility survey corridor. Given the lack of subsurface artifacts and good
ground visibility (80 to 90 percent), the site was delineated by the surface expression of artifacts
alone. Site delineation was limited to the 60-foot access/utility survey corridor. As a result, the
site may extend outside of the survey corridor. The site, as currently defined, measures 183
meters north to south by 106 meters east to west and has a total site area of 5,517 square meters.
Vegetation at the site is typical of thorn scrub brush lands (see Photograph B-74 and B-75).

41SR432 (3CarGarage-P-1)
The site was originally recorded from a surface scatter of artifacts. Three transect STPs (T1STP1 to T1-STP3) and three delineation STPs (D-1 to D-3) were excavated across the area of the
surface scatter, but none of the STPs were positive for cultural material (Table 6-14). A typical
shovel test profile for a STP excavated within the site boundary consisted of a dark yellowish
brown (10YR 4/4) silt loam to a depth of 50 centimeters below ground surface. None of the
shovel tests excavated was positive for cultural material. Given the excellent ground visibility
and lack of evidence of subsurface deposits, the site was defined by the surface expression of
artifacts. Site delineation was limited to the survey corridor and the site may extend beyond the
survey limits. As a result, the site boundaries, as currently defined, are considered incomplete.
The portion of the site recorded measures 59 meters north to south by 216 meters east to west
and has a total area of 3,521 square meters (see Figure A-58). The site is positioned on the
western slope of a very gently sloping upland hill. Vegetation at the site was dominated by an
understory of buffelgrass and an overstory of honey mesquite.

41SR428 (3CarGarage-A-1)
The site was originally recorded from the surface scatter of artifacts and one positive transect
STP (T1-STP3). An additional three STPs were excavated (D-1 to D-3), none of which were
positive for cultural material (Table 6-18; see Figure A-62). The site had good to excellent
surface visibility (60 percent) along its southern portion, near the top of the landform, but
reduced to poor (0 to 10 percent) surface visibility along the northern slope of the landforms (see
Photograph B-91). Given the limited subsurface material found, the site's boundaries were
largely determined by the surface expression of artifacts. Impacts on the site include an
unimproved road that cuts through the western portion of the site, a possible pipeline corridor
that cuts through the eastern portion of the site, and erosion of the hill. The soil profile of the
STP that was positive for cultural material consisted of a single stratum that was a very pale
brown (10YR 7/3) silty clay loam to a depth of 40 centimeters below ground surface. Vegetation
at the site consisted of a predominantly honey mesquite overstory and buffelgrass understory.

Artifacts recovered from the site included lithic debitage (six primary flakes, 16 secondary
flakes, 32 tertiary flakes, three angular debris), as well as stone tools (one core tool, two unhafted
bifaces tools, and one hafted biface [Matamoros projectile point]) (see Photographs B-92 and B93). The Matamoros projectile point has a fairly broad temporal association, dating the site from
the Late Archaic to Late Prehistoric periods. Given the artifact assemblage, the site probably
represents a short-term open campsite where limited resource processing was taking place.
While there is limited evidence of subsurface deposits, the site does contain stone tools, one of
which is diagnostic. As a result, the site may have the potential to address research questions
regarding prehistoric settlement and resource processing in south Texas. The archaeological
investigations were largely limited to the survey corridor and the site boundary is considered
incomplete, with additional portions of the site extending outside the current survey boundaries
to the north and east. As a result, additional archaeological investigations would be needed to
determine the eligibility of the site for the NRHP.

41SR429 (3CarGarage-A-2)
Site 41SR429 was originally recorded from a surface scatter of artifacts. Six STPs (D-1 to D-6)
were excavated during the delineation of the site, but none of the test pits excavated was positive
for cultural material (Table 6-19; see Figure A-63). Soil stratigraphy in the STPs consisted of a

The site occupies an eastward sloping upland flat that drops into a slight drainage that forms into
a tributary of Arroyo la Minita to the north. Vegetation at the site was minimal and consisted of
a predominantly honey mesquite overstory and buffelgrass understory. Disturbance noted at the
site includes an unimproved road, which cuts through the center of the site, and off-road vehicle
traffic, which seems to have displaced surface artifacts. Erosion has also impacted the site,
particularly in the eastern portion where it drops into a small north-south drainage.
Artifacts recorded at the site were limited to surface material and consisted of predominantly
lithic debitage (eight primary flakes, 13 secondary flakes, 21 tertiary flakes, and one angular
debris) and a limited number of non-diagnostic stone tools (one core tool, one biface fragment
[distal]). Given the site's limited artifact assemblage, the site probably represents a singleoccupation, limited-use, open campsite. Given the light density of artifacts, limited artifact
assemblage, and noted disturbance which adversely impacts the integrity of the site, the site has
little or no potential to provide additional information about prehistoric settlement and adaptation
in south Texas.

41SR431 (3CarGarage-A-5)
The site was originally recorded from a surface scatter of lithic debitage. An additional six
delineation STPs (D-1 to D-6) were excavated at the site, none of which were positive for
cultural material (Table 6-21; see Figure A-65). The soil profile from one of the excavated STPs
consisted of two strata. Stratum I consisted of a light yellowish brown (10YR 6/4) silt loam to a
depth of 30 cmbgs. Stratum II consisted of a very pale brown (10YR 7/4) weakly cemented
caliche. Surface visibility was 80 percent. Given the lack of subsurface deposits and the good
surface visibility, the site boundary was determined from the surface expression of artifacts. The
site boundaries are considered complete and measure 39 meters east to west by 13 meters north
to south and the site has a total site area of 310 square meters.

The site is positioned on an upland terrace that gently slopes east to a drainage that forms a
tributary of Ramirez Creek (see Photograph B-96). Vegetation at the site is dominated by an
overstory of honey mesquite and an understory of buffelgrass. Disturbance noted at the site was
limited to an unimproved road, which cuts through the center of the site. Approximately 60
percent of the site is considered intact.
Artifacts recovered from the site were limited to lithic debitage (one primary flake, three
secondary flakes, five tertiary flakes, and one piece of angular debris) of chert. Given the limited
artifact assemblage, the site probably represents a short-term, single-use tool modification site
and is considered to have little or no potential to answer questions about prehistoric adaptation
and settlement in south Texas.
IO-3CarGarage-A-3 (3CarGarage-A-3)

for cultural material (Table 6-22; Figure A-66). As a result, the locus consists of a single artifact
and is considered an IO. The IO is not considered an archaeological site and is not
recommended eligible for listing in the NRHP. No additional archaeological investigations are
recommended for IO-3CarGarage-A-3.

6.1.8.2 Historic Resource Investigations
Two aboveground/architectural historic resources, identified by GSRC personnel, were recorded
within the visual APE for the proposed RGC NW of Horse Corrals Fronton Primary tower site.
Old Fronton Cemetery (Cemetery ID No. SR-C046) (see Photograph B-105) is located
approximately 0.74 km (0.46 mile) northwest from the center of the tower site. It is
approximately 0.37 acres and is surrounded by a 5-foot-tall chain-link fence. Two chain-link
gates sit on the east (primary) side of the cemetery. Facing southeast, the tower site is not likely
to be visible from the east side of the cemetery due to tree coverage. As a result, no adverse
effects on architectural or aboveground resources are anticipated. New Fronton Cemetery
(Cemetery ID No. SR-C101) (see Photograph B-106) is located approximately 0.59 km (0.36
mile) northwest from the center of the tower site. It is approximately 0.94 acres and is
surrounded by a 5-foot-tall chain-link fence on the north, south, and east sides. On the west
(primary) side is a fence constructed with red brick pillars and metal poles. Facing southeast, the
tower site is likely to be visible from the west (primary) and east (rear) sides of the cemetery but
does not constitute an adverse visual element. As a result, no adverse effects on architectural or
aboveground resources are anticipated. Additional structures present within the APE consist of
small residential, utility, and commercial structures.

41SR440 (HorseCorral-A-2)
Site 41SR440 was originally recorded from a surface scatter of artifacts noted during the survey
of the tower site, as well as subsurface material recorded within a transect STP (T1-STP2). An
additional two delineation STPs were excavated (D-1 and D-2), of which one was positive for
cultural material (D-2) (Table 6-26; see Figure A-70). The soil profile from the positive
delineation shovel test consists of a pale brown (10YR 6/3) clay to a depth of 40 centimeters
below ground surface. The site is located in a recently plowed field that was fallow at the time
of the survey. As a result, no vegetation was present within the site boundary and surface
visibility was excellent (95 to 100 percent) across the site area. The site, as currently defined,
measures 38 meters north to south by 18 meters east to west and has a total site area of 538
square meters.

Artifacts recovered from the site include prehistoric lithic debitage (seven tertiary and two
shatter) of chert, as well as limited historic material (two glass shards). Prehistoric material was
found to a depth of 20 centimeters below ground surface in two excavated STPs that were

Cultural Resources Inventory and Impacts Report, Rio Grande City, McAllen, and
Weslaco Stations’ Areas of Responsibility Remote Video Surveillance Upgrade Project

Final

6-24

to a depth of 20 centimeters below ground surface. Heavy clays and compacted soils limited the
excavation depth of shovel tests to 40 centimeters below ground surface. Given the mixture of
historic and prehistoric material to a depth of 20 centimeters below ground surface, plowing
seems to have disturbed the majority of the site and only an estimated 5 to 10 percent of the site
remains intact. No stone tools, diagnostic cultural material, or cultural features were recorded
during the delineation of the site. The limited artifact assemblage suggests that the site represents
a short-term, single-use open campsite.
Given the very limited artifact assemblage and the evidence of disturbance noted in the shovel
tests, the site is considered to have poor integrity and has little or no potential to address research
questions regarding prehistoric or historic adaptation in south Texas.

41SR403
Site 41SR403 was originally identified during a plant survey that was conduct by the USFWS in
2015. The previously conducted work at site 41SR403 has been described in section 5.1.10.2.
During the current survey of the southern portion of the access and utility corridor GSRC
personnel recorded an extension of 41SR403, which was identified from one positive transect
STP (T3-STP1) and an extensive surface scatter of artifacts (see Figure A-72). The site extends
from the eastern 2015 boundary approximately 490 meters south and southeast along the current
access and utility corridor and extending across the northern portion of the tower footprint. The
recording of the extension of 41SR403 was largely limited to the current survey area and the site
encompasses the entire survey corridor and the northern portion of the tower pad. A surface
reconnaissance beyond the survey area noted artifacts extending to the east, west, and north of
the current survey area and the site boundaries of the extension of 41SR403 are not considered
complete. An additional four (T3-STP2 to T3STP5) transect STPs were excavated across along
the access and utility corridor within the surface scatter of artifacts, but none of them were
positive for cultural material (see Table 6-27). In addition, none of the STPs excavated at the
tower site were positive for cultural material.
The site is positioned on a relatively high ridge that drops into a large tributary of the Rio Grande
to the west, and several smaller drainages to the east. Vegetation at the site was typical of south
Cultural Resources Inventory and Impacts Report, Rio Grande City, McAllen, and
Weslaco Stations’ Areas of Responsibility Remote Video Surveillance Upgrade Project

Final
November 2016

6-26

Texas shrublands and consisted of predominantly honey mesquite overstory and buffelgrass
understory. Other vegetation noted near the site includes huisachillo (Acacia schaffneri),
limberbush (Jatropha cardiophylla), bee brush (Aloysia gratissima), five-needle dogweed
(Thymophylla pentachaeta), coyotillo (Karwinskia humboldtiana), guayacan (Guaiacum
angustifolium), huisache (Acacia farnesiana), and Texas ranger (Leucophyllum frutescens) (see
Photograph B-123). The soil profile from the single positive STP excavated within the site
boundary consists of a yellowish brown (10YR 5/4) clay to a depth of 50 cmbgs.
Artifacts were recorded predominantly from the surface with a single secondary flake of chert,
which was recovered from a depth of 25 cmbgs. The artifact inventory from the site was
dominated by lithic debitage (84 primary flakes, 121 secondary flakes, 162 tertiary flakes, and 73
pieces of angular debris). A limited number of stone tools were also recorded at the site and
included five core tools and four bifaces (see Photographs B-124 to B-133). Finally a small
amount of faunal material (11 shell) was also recorded within the site boundary. All of the lithic
material recorded was composed of chert. Chert colors included white, brown, and gray. Given
the artifact inventory recorded within the extension of the site, GSRC personnel concur with the
interpretation of the site in 2015 that it primarily represents a quarry and lithic procurement site.
The larger proportion of latter stage reduction debitage, as well as the presence of several biface
tools, tool production and resource processing may have also been conducted at the site. The
presence of unworked shell at the site may also suggest that subsistence processing may have
also taken place at the site.
Only a limited portion of the overall site was investigated during the current survey, as work was
limited to the survey boundaries of the access and utility corridor and tower footprint. Limited
reconnaissance outside of the current survey boundary noted that surface artifacts extended
toward the north, west, and east of the current survey area and the site boundaries are
incomplete. The site may have the potential to address multiple research questions regarding
resource procurement, tool manufacturing and possibly subsistence within south Texas.
Additional archaeological investigations would be required to assess the potential of site
41SR403 for inclusion in the NRHP. As a result, GSRC recommends that the site have an
undetermined eligibility for the NRHP until additional archaeological investigations can be
conducted, including more extensive mapping of the site boundaries and surface expression of
artifacts and possibly additional subsurface testing to determine if intact subsurface deposits exist
outside of the current survey corridor.

No previously recorded aboveground/architectural resources that are listed in the NRHP or
eligible for listing in the NRHP are located within the visual APE of the proposed RGC Papa
Hill Primary tower site and along its access and utility corridor. There are no visible structures
present in the APE.

41SR392
The site was previously recorded by e2M in 2007 during their survey of areas for the proposed
U.S. border fence and associated construction staging area. In 2007 they recorded 50 surface
artifacts and 99 subsurface artifacts. In shovel tests excavated at the site, they noted cultural
material to a depth of 1.4 meters below ground surface. The e2M recorder recommended that the
site be eligible for the NRHP and that it should be avoided or mitigated prior to construction of
the border fence. In 2007 the site was utilized as an observation point for CBP.
When GSRC revisited the site, CBP had erected a temporary remote video surveillance tower
within its boundaries. A built-up gravel platform was constructed for the placement of the
temporary remote video surveillance tower, with concrete anchors on the surface to secure and
stabilize the temporary tower. As a result, the establishment of the temporary remote video
surveillance tower resulted in little or no ground disturbance within the previously recorded site.
The current investigation utilized limited shovel test excavations (three STPs [T1-STP1 to T1STP3]) and surface inspection to relocate the site and verify that presence of the previously
recorded subsurface artifacts. Two of the three STPs excavated (T1-STP1 and T1-STP3) were
positive for cultural material to depths of 50 centimeters below ground surface (see Table 6-28;
see Figure A-74). Additional surface artifacts were noted and conformed in distribution to the
previously established site boundaries. No stone tools or diagnostic artifacts were noted during
the site revisit. Given the results of the site revisit, the condition of the site has largely remained
the same and GSRC concurs with the previous recommendations for the site.
41SR439 (PapaHill-3-1-1)
The site was originally recorded from a surface scatter of artifacts and a single positive transect
STP (T2-STP8). An additional five delineation STPs (D-1 to D-5) were excavated, but none
were positive for cultural material (Table 6-29; see Figure A- 75). As a result, the site boundary
was largely determined from the surface expression of artifacts. Site delineation was generally
kept within the limits of the survey corridor, though a surface reconnaissance noted artifacts
extending beyond the limits of the survey. As a result, the site boundary to the west is not
considered complete. The site boundaries to the north, south, and east are complete. The site, as
currently delineated, measures 56 meters north to south by 48 meters east to west and has a total
site area of 1,851 square meters. The site is positioned on a gently sloping upland terrace that
overlooks an abandoned tributary of Los Negros Creek. Vegetation at the site was dominated by
an overstory of honey mesquite and Texas ebony and an understory of predominantly buffelgrass
(see Photograph B-142).

Artifacts were recovered predominantly from the surface, though cultural material was recovered
to a depth of 20 centimeters below ground surface in the initial positive transect shovel test. The
soil profile of that positive shovel test consisted of a light yellowish brown (10YR 6/4) silty loam
to a depth of 35 centimeters below ground surface, before encountering a weakly cemented
limestone bedrock. Artifacts recorded at the site were dominated by chert lithic debitage in
predominantly later stage reduction (10 primary flakes, 26 secondary flakes, 45 tertiary flakes,
and six pieces of angular debris). One stone tool was recorded from the surface, which was a
multidirectional core tool (see Photographs B-143 and B-144). A minimal amount (two
fragments) of thermally altered rock fragments were also recorded from the surface, which may
indicate the presence of unidentified thermal features at the site. Given the artifact assemblage,
the site probably represents a short-term, open campsite of unknown prehistoric cultural
affiliation. While the artifact assemblage lacks substantial stone tools and diagnostic artifacts,
the minimal presence of thermally altered rock at the site may indicate that unrecorded thermal
features may be present at the site. Given that a large portion of the site lies outside the current
survey corridor to the west and is unrecorded, the eligibility of the site for the NRHP cannot be
determined without additional archaeological investigations.
IO-PapaHill-3-1 (PapaHill-3-1)
IO-PapaHill-3-1 consists of a single concrete feature with no associated artifacts. The feature
consisted of a possible rectangular concrete watering trough for cattle (see Photograph B-145).
The trough was oriented southeast to northwest and measured 237 cm in length by 131 cm in
width. The walls of the trough were 20 cm thick. The exterior height of the feature walls were
65 cm while the interior height of the walls were approximately 50 cm in height. Given that the
locus consisted of an isolated feature with no associated artifact scatter it is considered to be an
IO. IO-PapaHill-3-1 is not considered eligible for listing in the NRHP and no further
archaeological work is recommended for the IO.
IO-PapaHill-3-2 (PapaHill-3-2)
IO-PapaHill-3-2 was originally recorded from a single positive transect STP (T2-STP11), which
recovered a single secondary flake from 20 to 30 cmbgs. An additional five delineation STPs
(D-1 to D-5) were excavated at the locus, but none of the STPs were positive for cultural
material (Table 6-30; see Figure A-77). Given that only a single artifact was recovered from the
locus it is considered to be an IO (see Photograph B-146). IO-PapaHill-3-2 is not considered to
be eligible for listing in the NRHP and no additional archaeological investigations are
recommended for the IO.

6.1.12 RGC Relay Tower
6.1.12.1 Archaeological Resource Investigations
On the eastern portion of the access and utility corridor the existing unimproved access road is
immediately bounded to the south by a barbed wire fence and property boundary and to the north
by a wide pipeline corridor. As a result, the excavation of STPs was not possible along an 880meter portion along the access utility corridor from the tower footprint west. Three STPs (T1STP1 to T1-STP3) were excavated within the tower footprint and 22 STPs (T2-STP1 to T2STP22) were excavated along the remaining portion of the access and utility corridor during the
survey of the RGC Relay Tower and its associated access and utility corridor (see Photographs
B-148 to B-155; see Figure A-79). One of the STPs was positive for cultural material (T2STP14) and three surface scatters of artifacts were identified during the surveys (Table 6-32).
This resulted in the recording of three new archaeological sites (41SR425, 41SR426, and
41SR427).

41SR425 (RelayTower-1)
Site 41SR425 was originally recorded from a surface find of a chert secondary flake found near a
transect STP (T1-STP3). No subsurface cultural material was noted in any of the transect STPs
excavated and surface visibility across the site was excellent (90 to 95 percent). In addition, the
depositional environment and soil type present at the site suggests that there is little or no
potential for subsurface deposits at the site. Given the factors above, the delineation of the
boundaries of the site was determined from the surface expression of artifacts alone and no
additional delineation STPs were excavated (see Figure A-80). The site is positioned on the
gentle northern and western slopes of an upland hill (see Photograph B-156). The site measures
49 meters north to south by 35 meters east to west and has a total site area of 929 square meters.
Vegetation at the site was sparse and dominated by a honey mesquite overstory and buffelgrass
understory. Soils at the site were shallow and consistent with the typical pedon of the JimenezQuemado Association soil type. The soil profile from transect STP 1-3 was composed of two
strata. Stratum I consisted of a yellowish brown (10YR 5/6) loam with heavy gravel and cobbles
(60 percent) to a depth of 25 centimeters below ground surface, followed by Stratum II, a weakly

Cultural Resources Inventory and Impacts Report, Rio Grande City, McAllen, and
Weslaco Stations’ Areas of Responsibility Remote Video Surveillance Upgrade Project

Final
November 2016

6-33

cemented caliche from 25 to 35 centimeters below ground surface. The limits of the site could
be determined in all directions and the site boundary is considered complete.
The artifact assemblage at the site was extremely light and consisted of lithic debitage (one
primary flake and three secondary flakes), as well as stone tools (three multidirectional core
tools) of chert (see Photographs B-157 to B-160). The artifact assemblage suggests that the site
represents a very limited-use, lithic procurement area of unknown cultural affiliation. Given the
lack of subsurface deposits, lack of diagnostic artifacts and features, and low artifact density, the
site is considered to have little or no potential to address research questions regarding prehistoric
settlement, tool production, or lithic procurement in south Texas.
41SR426 (RelayTower-2)
Site 41SR426 was originally recorded from a positive transect STP (T2-STP14), which
recovered material between 20 and 30 centimeters below ground surface. An additional four
delineation STPs (D-1 to D-4) were excavated, none of which were positive for cultural material
(Table 6-33; see Figure A-81). An extensive surface scatter of material was also noted across the
site area, increasing in density towards the north and west, outside of the current survey corridor.
As a result, the site boundary was determined through a combination of surface expression of
artifacts and excavation of delineation and transect STPs. The site boundary is complete on the
eastern, western, and southern edges. A surface reconnaissance conducted outside of the current
survey corridor to the north noted a large amount of cultural material extending in that direction.
As a result, the site boundary in that direction is not considered complete. As currently
delineated, the site measures 136 meters northwest to southeast along an unnamed dirt road by
79 meters southwest to northeast and has a total site area of 5,184 square meters.

STP excavated at site 41SR426 consisted of a single stratum of very pale brown (10YR 7/4)

Artifacts recorded at the site were predominantly from the surface. Artifacts recorded were
overwhelmingly dominated by lithic debitage (10 primary flakes, 27 secondary flakes, 65 tertiary
flakes, and four pieces of angular debris) of chert. Only a single stone tool was recorded at the
site, which consisted of a distal biface fragment of chert (see Photographs B-162 and B-163).
The remaining assemblage consisted of a single specimen of thermally altered rock. Subsurface
artifacts were only recovered from a single transect STP and consisted of one secondary flake
and one tertiary flake recovered from 20 to 30 centimeters below ground surface. Given the
artifact assemblage, the site probably represents a limited, short-term prehistoric open campsite
of unknown cultural affiliation. While the site has a fairly limited artifact assemblage within the
current survey corridor, a large portion of the site extends northward and additional
archaeological investigations would be needed to determine the site's eligibility for the NRHP.
41SR427 (RelayTower-3)

the surface scatter of artifacts to determine the boundaries of the site (Table 6-34). None of the
delineation STPs was positive for cultural material. The single transect STP (T2-STP20) that
was excavated within the site boundary was also negative for cultural material. A surface
reconnaissance outside of the survey corridor noted only a limited number of artifacts extending
beyond the survey corridor that were mapped and the site boundaries are considered complete
(see Figure A-82). The site, as delineated, measures 117 meters east to west by 39 meters north
to south, and has a total site area of 2,352 square meters.

All the artifacts recorded from the site came from the surface. The artifact assemblage was

6.1.13.2 Historic Resource Investigations
The boundary of Roma NRHP-listed historic district (Reference No. 72001371) is located within
the visual APE of the proposed RGC Igloo House Primary tower site. The boundaries of the
historic district include 11 major architecturally significant structures by Enrique Portscheller, as
well as stone and adobe buildings typical of the Rio Grande Valley. Also included in the visual
APE are nine OTHM. The Roma City Hall marker (Marker No. 4341), the Noah Cox House
marker (Marker No. 6055), the Old Garcia Home marker (Marker No. 3734), the Our Lady of
Refuge Catholic Church marker (Marker No. 3885), the Old Roma Convent Building marker
(Marker No. 4342), and the Knights of Columbus Hall marker (Marker No. 6058) are all
medallion markers. The Early Commercial Center marker (Marker No. 6062) outlines the
history of the indigenous Mexican-style building. The Manuel Guerra Store marker (Marker No.
3200) details the construction of the classic-revival structure. The Memorial Hospital marker

Cultural Resources Inventory and Impacts Report, Rio Grande City, McAllen, and
Weslaco Stations’ Areas of Responsibility Remote Video Surveillance Upgrade Project

Final
November 2016

6-36

(Marker No. 3324) details the construction of the building and its function. These three OTHMs
are medallions with plaques.
The proposed RGC Igloo House Primary tower site is visible from all historic resources present
in the APE and, while it does constitute an adverse visual element, the resources present would
not be directly impacted because the use of adjacent properties has changed dramatically. In
addition, adverse visual elements are already present within the visual APE. As a result, no
adverse effects on architectural or aboveground resources that are eligible for the NRHP are
anticipated. Other structures present within the visual APE are a combination of large and small
commercial and residential buildings.

6.1.17 RGC North of Dairy Pump Primary
6.1.17.1 Archaeological Resource Investigations

6.1.17.2 Historic Resource Investigations
Two aboveground/architectural historic resources were recorded within the visual APE of the
proposed RGC North of Dairy Pump Primary tower site. Los Garzas Cemetery (Cemetery ID
No. SR-C045) (see Photograph B-212) is located approximately 0.37 km (0.23 mile) southeast
from the center of the tower site. No established date is known for the cemetery. It is
approximately 0.21 acres and is not surrounded by a fence of any kind. Facing northwest, the
tower site is likely to be visible from the west (primary) side of the cemetery, but it will not
constitute as an adverse visual element. The Unknown Grave (Cemetery ID No. SR-C044) (see
Photograph B-213) is located 0.61 km (0.38 mile) east of the center of the tower site. No
established date is known for the cemetery. It is approximately 0.3 acre and is surrounded by
vegetation. At the center of the plot of land is a single gravestone that reads “N. Los Garzas.”
Facing west, the tower site is unlikely to be visible from the west (primary) side of the cemetery
due to tree coverage. As a result, no adverse effects on aboveground historic resources are
anticipated. Other existing structures present within the visual APE include both residential and
commercial structures.

41SR442 (DairyPump-A-2)
Site 41SR442 was originally recorded from a surface find near transect STP T2-STP6. An
additional five STPs (D-1 to D-5) were excavated during the delineation of the site, none of
which were positive for cultural material (Table 6-41). Surface visibility at the site was
excellent, with 95 to 100 percent surface visibility across the site. Given the lack of any
evidence of subsurface deposits and the overall excellent surface visibility, the boundaries of the
site were determined from the surface expression of artifacts alone. No additional cultural
material was noted extending beyond the survey corridor. As a result, the site boundary is
considered complete. The site measures 37 meters northwest to southeast by 12 meters northeast
to southwest, and has a total surface area of 325 square meters (see Figure A-89).

The site is situated on an escarpment that overlooks the Rio Grande floodplain to the south. The
area around the site has seen heavy modification for gravel or caliche extraction. Vegetation
across the site was sparse, and consisted of an overstory dominated by honey mesquite and an
understory dominated by buffelgrass (see Photograph B-222). The soil profile from the transect
STP located near the center of the site consisted of a single stratum of brown (10YR 5/3) sandy
loam to a depth of 76 centimeters below ground surface.
The artifact assemblage recorded at the site was sparse and consisted of lithic debitage (two
tertiary flakes and one angular debris) and stone tools (two unimarginal flake tools) (see
Photographs B-223 to B-226). All cultural material was recorded from the surface and no
subsurface cultural material was noted in any of the STPs excavated at the site. The limited
artifact assemblage suggests that the site represents a short-term, single-occupation, open
campsite. Given the limited artifact assemblage, as well as the questionable integrity, the site is
considered to have little or no potential to provide significant information on prehistoric
settlement, resource processing, or adaptation in south Texas.
41SR441 (DairyPump-A-1)
Site 41SR441 was originally recorded from two positive transect STPs (2-1 and 2-2). An
additional four delineation STPs (D-1 to D-4) were excavated during the site delineation, of
which three were positive for cultural material (Table 6-42). In addition to subsurface cultural
material, surface artifacts were noted within the site boundaries, with the heaviest concentration
in the southern portion of the site found within an artifact concertation mapped as AC-2 (see
Figure A-90). Site delineation was limited to the east by a property fence line and to the west by
the survey corridor boundary. This limited the excavation of STPs along that portion of the site
to a single line paralleling the road. Surface visibility at the site was poor (5 percent), with the
exception of the unimproved dirt road running through the eastern portion of the site, which
provided good surface visibility. Given the limitation of the survey corridor, the site boundary is
not considered complete and may extend further to the east or west. As currently defined, the site
measures 191 meters northeast to southwest by 17 meters northwest to southeast and has a total
site area of 3,064 square meters.

II (30-50 cmbgs) 10YR 6/6 sand clay

The site is positioned on a relatively flat and broad upland ridge that drops into the Arroyo
Quiote drainage to the west. Vegetation at the site had a limited overstory of honey mesquite,
which was concentrated along the property line on the eastern boundary of the site. The
understory was a thick ground cover of buffelgrass, creating poor surface visibility across the
majority of the site (see Photograph B-227). A profile from a positive STP excavated at the site
consisted of two strata. Stratum I consisted of a yellowish brown (10YR 5/4) sandy loam which
extended to a depth of 70 centimeters below ground surface, followed by Stratum II, which
consisted of a light yellowish brown (10YR 6/4) compact and loosely cemented caliche.
Artifacts recorded at the site included both surface and subsurface cultural material. Subsurface
cultural material was recorded to a depth of 80 centimeters below ground surface. Artifacts
recorded included lithic debitage (four primary flakes, nine secondary flakes, 22 tertiary flakes,
and four pieces of angular debris) of chert, chalcedony, and quartzite and multiple chert stone
tools (two unimarginal flake tools, three core tools, two biface fragments) (see Photographs B228 to B-245). In addition to the flaked stone, fragments of thermally altered rock were recorded
in the STPs excavated in the northern portion of the site, which suggests that unidentified
thermal features may be present at the site. Given the artifact assemblage, the site probably
represents a single-occupation, open campsite. The presence of multiple stone tools and
thermally altered rocks suggests that resource processing was taking place at the site. Given the
artifact assemblage and presence of intact subsurface deposits, the site has the potential to
provide significant information about prehistoric settlement, resource processing including
subsistence data, and prehistoric adaptation in south Texas. Given that the investigation was
limited to the 60-foot survey corridor and only examined a portion of the site, additional
archaeological investigations would be needed to evaluate the site for inclusion in the NRHP.

tower are a mixture of residential and commercial buildings.

6.1.22.2 Historic Resource Investigations
No aboveground/architectural historic resources were identified within the visual APE of the
proposed RGC Tower near Silos RGC Primary tower site. The boundary of Fort Ringgold
Historic District NRHP-listed historic district (Reference No. 93000196) intersects with the
visual APE of the proposed tower site. It is estimated that the proposed tower site will have
minimal visual impact on Fort Ringgold Historic District NRHP-listed historic district, as there
are no contributing elements of the historic district that would be directly impacted. The closest
historic site is 0.83 km (0.51 mile) away. As a result, no adverse effects on architectural or
aboveground historic resources that are eligible for the NRHP are anticipated. Other existing
structures present within the visual APE of the tower site are a combination of large and small
commercial and residential buildings.
6.1.23 RGC Tower Near Silos Alternate
6.1.23.1 Archaeological Resource Investigations
One STP (T3-STP1) was excavated along the access and utility corridor for the proposed RGC
Tower Near Silos Alternate tower site. The area encompassing the tower footprint itself was
heavily disturbed with extant concrete structures present across the tower site (see Photographs
B-274 to B-281). The heavy disturbance and built environment prevented the excavation of
STPs within the footprint of the proposed RGC Tower Near Silos Alternate tower site. No
archaeological resources were recorded within the STP excavated or during the pedestrian survey
of the proposed tower site and associated access road (Table 6-48).

6.1.23.2 Historic Resource Investigations
One OTHM, identified by GSRC personnel, is recorded within the visual APE of the proposed
RGC Tower near Silos RGC Alternate tower site. The OTHM is the Lee House marker (Marker
No. 6059). The marker is a medallion. The proposed RGC Tower near Silos RGC Alternate
tower site is visible from this historic site and, while it does constitute as an adverse visual
element, the resource would not be directly impacted because the use of adjacent properties has
changed dramatically, as well as already present adverse visual elements within the visual APE.
As a result, no adverse effects on architectural or aboveground resources that are eligible for the
NRHP are anticipated. The boundary of Fort Ringgold Historic District NRHP-listed historic
district (Reference No. 93000196) intersects with the visual APE of the RGC Tower near Silos
RGC Alternate proposed tower site. It is estimated that the proposed tower site will have
minimal visual impact on Fort Ringgold Historic District NRHP-listed historic district because
the use of adjacent properties has changed dramatically. As a result, no adverse effects on
architectural or aboveground resources that are eligible for the NRHP are anticipated. Other
existing structures present within the visual APE of the tower site are a combination of large and
small commercial and residential buildings.
6.1.24 RGC Los Velas Tertiary Option 1
6.1.24.1 Archaeological Resource Investigations
Three STPs (T1-STP1 to T1-STP3) were excavated within the tower footprint and seven STPs
(T2-STP1 to T2-STP7) were excavated along the access corridor during the survey of the RGC
Los Velas Tertiary Option 1 tower site (see Photographs B-282 to B-289). A 1,520-meter
portion of the access corridor has buried gas pipelines paralleling both side of the existing
roadway which encompassed the entire access corridor. This portion was excluded from
subsurface testing and the survey was limited to a pedestrian surface inspection. None of the
STPs were positive for archaeological material and no archaeological resources were recorded
during the survey of the RGC Los Velas Tertiary Option 1 tower site and its associated access
corridor (Table 6-49).

6.1.24.2 Historic Resource Investigations
No previously recorded aboveground/architectural historic resources that are listed in the NRHP
or eligible for listing in the NRHP are located within the visual APE of the proposed RGC Los
Velas Tertiary Option 1 tower site. The existing structures present within the visual APE are
utility facilities.
The Los Velas Soldier’s cemetery (Cemetery No. SR-C074) is not located within the visual APE
of the tower but is located on the western side of the access road (Los Velas Road),
approximately 1.45 km (0.9 mile) northwest from the center of the proposed tower site. The
cemetery is approximately 0.03 acre and is surrounded by a 3-foot chain-link fence on all four
sides. No additional information is known about the cemetery. The access road is not likely to
disturb the cemetery in any way, as it is located on private property.
6.1.25 RGC Los Velas Tertiary Option 2
6.1.25.1 Archaeological Resource Investigations
Three STPs (T1-STP1 to T1-STP3) were excavated within the tower footprint and 21 STPs (T2STP1 to T2-STP21) were excavated along the access and utility corridor during the survey of the
RGC Los Velas Tertiary Option 2 tower site and its associated access and utility corridor (see
Photographs B-290 to B-297). Surface visibility along the access and utility corridor was
excellent, 90 to 99 percent, with agricultural fields that were plowed and had experienced rain.
None of the STPs were positive for archaeological material and no archaeological resources were
recorded during the survey of the RGC Los Velas Tertiary Option 2 tower site and its associated
access and utility corridor (Table 6-50; see Figure A-98).

41SR443 (LaCasita-P-1)
The site was originally located from a surface scatter of historic material on the east side of
South Casita Farm Road. The site is positioned on a terrace overlooking the Rio Grande
floodplain. Vegetation noted at the site includes honey mesquite (Prosopis glandulosa),
bermuda grass (Cynodon dactylon), buffelgrass (Cenchrus ciliaris), tree tobacco (Nicotiana
glauca), Russian thistle (Salsola tragus), Palmer's amaranth (Amaranthus palmeri), and
silverleaf nightshade (Solanum elaeagnifolium). Surface visibility at the site varied between 5
and 90 percent across the site (see Photograph B-322).
Given the potential for subsurface deposits and the poor ground visibility over some portions of
the site area, subsurface testing through the excavation of STPs was also conducted at the site.
An additional six STPs (D-1 to D-6) were excavated at the site, of which three were positive for
cultural material (Table 6-53). The soil profiles from the positive shovel tests were not typical of
the McAllen Sandy Loam soil type and may represent fill material brought in. A typical soil
Cultural Resources Inventory and Impacts Report, Rio Grande City, McAllen, and
Weslaco Stations’ Areas of Responsibility Remote Video Surveillance Upgrade Project

Final

profile from a positive STP consisted of a pale brown (10YR 6/3) clay loam to a depth of 50
centimeters below ground surface. Site delineation was limited by the survey corridor to the
east, which only allowed a single line of STPs to be excavated parallel to the road. As a result,
the site boundary to the east is not considered complete. The site, as currently delineated,
measures 71 meters southwest to northeast by 10 meters northwest to southeast and has a total
site area of 660 square meters (see Figure A-101).

Artifacts recorded from the surface and within STPs consisted of ceramics, including decorated
and undecorated whiteware, decorated and undecorated porcelain, undecorated stoneware, and
annular yellowware. Glass artifacts recorded included curved and bottle glass shards in various
colors including clear, amber, green, yellow, black (dark olive), and solarized manganese (see
Photographs B-323 to B-329). One clear glass bottle basal shard had an Illinois Glass Company
maker's mark of an "I" within a diamond (see Photograph B-329). The “I” within a diamond
maker’s mark was used from 1916 to 1929 (Toulouse 2001:264). Metal artifacts recorded
consisted of ferrous metal artifacts including wire nails and fence staples, and non-ferrous metal
fragments including aluminum foil and tin. Other artifacts included brick fragments, a cement
fragment, and a limited number of indeterminate faunal bone fragments. One possible feature
(Feature 1 [F.1]) was recorded at the site, which was a 1- to 3-foot-high earthen embankment
located in the central portion of the site and extending outside of the survey corridor to the east.
Heavy grasses obscured most of its surface, making the exact mapping of the rise difficult. The
rise may be a raised earthen foundation for a structure. The road located to the west of the site
seems to have been widened and may have impacted the western portion of the site.

Cultural Resources Inventory and Impacts Report, Rio Grande City, McAllen, and
Weslaco Stations’ Areas of Responsibility Remote Video Surveillance Upgrade Project

November 2016

6-54

Archival maps consulted show three structures on the east side of the road in the vicinity of the
site on the 1965 La Grulla, TX 7.5-minute topographic quadrangle. The 1956 Garcias, TX 15minute topographic quadrangle shows one structure in the vicinity of the site and it is marked as
a school. No earlier maps of sufficient scale to see structures were found during the archival
research.
Given the artifact assemblage and limited archival map evidence, the site probably represents
one or more farmsteads that were present in the area with an occupation dating from the late
nineteenth to middle twentieth century. Given the large number of turn-of-the-century farmstead
archaeological sites already investigated within south Texas, site 41SR443 has a relatively poor
potential to provide additional information regarding turn of the century historic settlement and
adaptation in south Texas. The site is not recommended as eligible for inclusion in the NRHP
and no additional archaeological investigations are recommended for 41SR443.
6.1.27.2 Historic Resource Investigations
No previously recorded aboveground/architectural historic resources that are listed in the NRHP
or eligible for listing in the NRHP are located within the visual APE of the proposed RGC La
Casita Main Primary tower site and along its access road. The existing structures within the
visual APE of the tower site are mostly cylindrical concrete silos.
6.1.28 RGC La Casita Main Alternate
6.1.28.1 Archaeological Resource Investigations
An existing unimproved road and natural gas pipelines encompassed the entire footprint of the
associated 30-foot access roadway and the tower footprint itself was heavily disturbed by oil and
gas development. Overall surface visibility across the tower footprint and access road was good
to excellent, 70 to 90 percent (see Photographs B-330 to B-333). Given the heavy disturbance
and overall good surface visibility, both the tower footprint and access road were subject to an
intensive pedestrian survey and surface inspection. Three STPs were excavated at the tower
footprint, all of which were negative for cultural material (Table 6-54; see Figure A-102). No
archaeological resources were recorded during the survey of the RGC La Casita Main Alternate
tower site and associated access road.

41SR420 (Silvertanks-P-1)
The site was originally recorded from a surface scatter of artifacts noted around a transect STP
(T2-STP6). An additional four transect STPs were excavated across the site, but none were
positive for subsurface cultural material (see Figure A-105). One additional delineation STP was
excavated to the east of transect STP T2-STP6, to further test for subsurface artifacts downslope
of the ridge, but was also negative for subsurface material (Table 6-58). The soil profile of the
delineation STP near the center of the site consisted of single stratum of brown (10YR 5/3) loam
with a heavy cobble and gravel concentration to a depth of 50 centimeters below ground surface.
The profiles noted in the STPs excavated across the site and the heavy concentration of cobbles
Cultural Resources Inventory and Impacts Report, Rio Grande City, McAllen, and
Weslaco Stations’ Areas of Responsibility Remote Video Surveillance Upgrade Project

Final

6-58

and gravel on the surface was more typical of the Jimenez-Quemado association pedon, which
seems to be the dominant soil type across the site, instead of either Ramadero Loam (Ra) or
McAllen fine sandy loam (Mc).

41SR420 (Silvertanks-P-1).

Field Site 2; ~ 15 meters east of STP 2-6; high

The site extends along a ridgetop and eastern slope of a southwest-to-northeast trending ridge. A
barbed wire fence and property line runs along the top of the ridge and forms the western
boundary of the site. To the east the land drops to a southwest-to-northeast abandoned channel
that was a tributary of Salado Creek. The ridge and slope have exposed cobbles and gravel on
the surface. While the area is mapped as being predominantly Ramadero Loam and McAllen
Fine Sandy Loam, actual surface corresponds better with the typical pedon of the JiminezQuemado Association soil type. Vegetation at the site is dominated by an understory of
buffelgrass (Cenchrus ciliare) and a scattered overstory of honey mesquites (Prosopis
glandulosa), Texas paloverde (Parkinsonia texanum), huisache (Acacia farnesiana), blackbrush
acacia (Acacia rigidula), spiny hackberry (Celtis pallida), and wild lime (Zanthoxylum fagara)
(see Photograph B-349).
Given the concentration of cobbles and gravel on the surface, the absence of any subsurface
artifacts across the site, and the good to excellent ground surface visibility, the surface
expression of artifacts was utilized to determine the site boundary. The boundary of the site to
the west was set at the barbed wire fence line which is thought to be the property boundary. Site
delineation was largely limited to the survey corridor, which extended 60 feet east from the fence
line. As a result, the site boundary is not considered complete and may extend further to both the
west and east, outside of the current survey area. The site, as currently defined, measures 398
meters northeast to southwest by 28 meters northwest to southeast and has a total site area of
7,505 square meters.
Artifacts recovered consisted of lithic debitage (83 primary flakes, 23 secondary flakes, 15
tertiary flakes, 19 tested cobbles, and four angular debris), flake stone tools (24 cores
multidirectional and unidirectional), and ground stone tools (two possible manos and three
possible metates) (see Photographs B-350 to B-365). All lithic material consisted of chert.
Given the predominance of early stage reduction debitage and the high percentage of cores, the
site probably represents a repeated-use, primary reduction locus or quarry site.
Given the large amount of artifacts recorded at the site, the site has the potential to provide
information on lithic procurement, lithic sourcing, and prehistoric adaptations in the area. The
current survey only recorded the portion of the site within the current survey corridor and
additional archaeological investigations would be needed to assess the site's eligibility for the
NRHP.

41SR421 (Silvertanks-P-2)
Site 41SR421 was originally recorded from a surface scatter of artifacts noted around a transect
STP (T2-STP10). An additional four delineation STPs (D-1 to D-4) were excavated across the
site, with one STP being positive for cultural material to a depth of 30 cmbgs (Table 6-59). The
boundary of the site to the west was set at the barbed wire fence line, which is thought to be the
property boundary. Site delineation was largely limited to the survey corridor, which extended
60 feet east from the fence line. The site boundary is considered complete, as additional material
was not noted outside of the survey corridor. The site measures 23 meters north to south by 16
meters east to west and has a total site area of 283 square meters (see Figure 106).

The soil profile of the positive delineation STP consisted of a single stratum of brown (10YR

concentration of cobbles and gravel on the surface were more typical of the Jimenez-Quemado
association pedon, which seems to be the dominant soil type across the site, instead of Ramadero
Loam (Ra).
The site is located in an abandoned channel of a tributary of Salado Creek, between two ridges
that contain larger lithic procurement/quarry sites. Site delineation was limited to the west by a
barbed wire fence and property line, and to the east by the limits of the survey corridor. While
the site is positioned within an abandoned channel between two higher landforms, the soil type is
consistent with the tops of the landforms and has a heavy cobble and gravel content in the upper
stratum. Vegetation at the site is dominated by an understory of buffelgrass (Cenchrus ciliare)
and a scattered overstory of honey mesquites (Prosopis glandulosa), Texas paloverde
(Parkinsonia texanum), huisache (Acacia farnesiana), blackbrush acacia (Acacia rigidula), spiny
hackberry (Celtis pallida), and wild lime (Zanthoxylum fagara) (see Photograph B-366).
Artifacts recovered consist of lithic debitage (eight primary flakes, two secondary flakes, four
tertiary flakes, and one piece of angular debris) and two flaked stone tools (one multidirectional
core tool and one unidirectional core tool) (see Photographs B-367 and B-368). All lithic
material consisted of chert. Given the predominance of early stage reduction debitage and the

Weslaco Stations’ Areas of Responsibility Remote Video Surveillance Upgrade Project
