November 2016

6-60

larger quarry sites located to the north and south of this site, this site probably also represents a
limited-use, lithic procurement/quarry site.
Given the limited number of artifacts recovered from the site and the lack of diagnostic material
and features, the site is considered to have a low research potential, particularly compared to the
larger lithic procurement sites found to the north and south. The site is recommended not
eligible for the NRHP.
41SR422 (SilverTanks-P-3)
Site 41SR422 was initially identified from surface artifacts noted in the vicinity of several
transect STPs excavated across the site area. None of the nine transect shovel tests pits
excavated across the site were positive for subsurface cultural material. Given the lack of
subsurface cultural material in the initial transect STPs, the depositional environment, soil type,
and good to excellent ground visibility, the site boundary was delineated utilizing surface
expression of artifacts alone. The western boundary of the site was set at the barbed wire fence
line, which is thought to be the property boundary. The site boundary is not considered complete
and may extend to both the west and east beyond the current survey area. As currently defined,
the site measures 569 meters northeast to southwest by 24 meters northwest to southeast and has
a total site area of 12,203 square meters (see Figure 107).
The site extends across several low east-west trending broad finger ridges separated by
intermittent narrow arroyos that drain into an abandoned tributary of Salado Creek. The main
abandoned channel of the tributary is to the west, outside of the current survey area. Vegetation
across the site area is sparse and consists of a limited overstory dominated by honey mesquite
(Prosopis glandulosa) and a very light understory of buffelgrass (Cenchrus ciliare) (see
Photograph B-369).
Artifacts recorded from the surface were predominantly chert lithic debitage from early stage
reduction (107 primary flakes, 45 secondary flakes, 32 tertiary flakes, 30 tested cobbles, and nine
angular debris). Stone tools recovered were limited to nine unidirectional and multidirectional
core tools of chert (see Photographs B-370 to B-372). No diagnostic artifacts or features were
recorded during the delineation of the site. While the potential for subsurface artifacts is
limited, the site does contain a large number of artifacts related to lithic procurement. The
amount of material recorded suggests that the site may have the potential to address research
questions regarding prehistoric lithic procurement and lithic sourcing in south Texas. It is
recommended that the site undergo additional testing to determine its eligibility for the NRHP.
41SR423 (SilverTanks-P-4)
Site 41SR423 was originally recorded from a single positive transect STP (T2-STP1). Ground
visibility at the site was poor to fair (5 to 20 percent) and the site boundaries were determined
through the excavation of an additional five delineation STPs (D-1 to D-5) (see Figure A-108).
Site delineation was largely limited to the survey corridor, which only allowed for a single line
of delineation shovel tests to be excavated paralleling the west side of the road. Two of the
additional five STPs that were excavated during the delineation of 41SR423 were positive for
cultural material (Table 6-60). The site boundary is considered incomplete and the site may
extend further to the west. To the east the site is bounded by a road, as well as a developed

The site is situated on an upper terrace that overlooks an abandoned channel of a tributary of
Salado Creek to the east. Vegetation at the site is dominated by honey mesquite in the overstory
and buffelgrass in the understory. Other vegetation noted near the site includes Texas paloverde
(Cercidium texanum), huisache (Acacia farnesiana), blackbrush acacia (Acacia rigidula), spiny
hackberry (Celtis ehrenbergiana), and wild lime (Zanthoxylum fagara) (see Photograph B-373).
Artifacts recovered at the site include both prehistoric and historic material. Prehistoric material
was limited to lithic debitage (one primary flake, one secondary flake, 10 tertiary flakes, and one
angular debris). No stone tools or features were recorded at site 41SR423. None of the historic
material recorded could be definitively dated to 50 years old or older and included glass (one
clear curved glass, one aquamarine curved glass) and a single ceramic (one whiteware sherd).
Modern material such as plastic, colorless glass bottles, and chicken wire, was scattered across
the surface of the site. Artifacts were recovered to a depth of 50 centimeters below ground
surface. One delineation shovel test recorded historic material mixed with prehistoric material to
a depth of 50 centimeters below ground surface, which suggests that the deposits may lack
integrity. No diagnostic artifacts or features were recorded during the delineation of the site.
The soil profile of the site consisted of a single stratum of brown (10YR 5/3) silt loam to a depth
of 50 centimeters below ground surface.

Cultural Resources Inventory and Impacts Report, Rio Grande City, McAllen, and
Weslaco Stations’ Areas of Responsibility Remote Video Surveillance Upgrade Project

6-62

While there seems to be some subsurface material present at the site, including historic and
prehistoric material, there is evidence of potential mixing of the prehistoric and historic deposits.
As a result, the site is considered to have a low integrity of deposits. The artifact assemblage
recorded at the site is also limited. Given the site's lack of integrity and limited amount of
recorded cultural material, the site is considered to have little or no potential to address questions
regarding the prehistoric or historic settlement and adaptations in south Texas.

41SR424 (SilverTanks-A-1)
The site was originally recorded from a single positive transect STP (2-6), which recorded
cultural material to a depth of 60 centimeters below ground surface. An additional five
delineation STPs (D-1 to D-4) were excavated, of which one was positive for cultural material
(Table 6-62; see Figure A-110). The soil profile for the positive transect STP consisted of a
brown (7.5YR 5/3) silt loam with gravel to a depth of 20 centimeters below ground surface, a
brown (7.5YR 4/3) silt loam with gravel from 30 to 50 centimeters below ground surface, and a
brown (7.5YR 5/3) silty clay loam from 50 to 80 centimeters below ground surface. The site is
positioned on a slightly sloping but relatively flat upland overlooking the Rio Grande floodplain
to the south. Vegetation at the site was dominated by a sparse overstory of honey mesquite and
an understory of buffelgrass. Ground visibility varied across the site with poor visibility (10 to
20 percent) in the grassy areas beside the road and two-tracks, and good visibility within the
roads and two-tracks (70 to 90 percent) (see Photograph B-381).

suggesting that the site does not extend to that side of the road. A two-track within the pipeline
corridor did have some cultural material recorded from the surface on the east side of the road.
Given the limits of the survey corridor, the site boundary is not considered complete. The site, as
currently defined, measures 47 meters northeast to southwest by 7 meters northwest to southeast
and has a total site area of 295 square meters.
Artifacts recovered from the site were limited to chert lithic debitage of latter stage reduction (10
primary, eight secondary, and 24 tertiary flakes). No stone tools, diagnostic cultural material, or
features were recorded during the delineation of the site. Cultural material was recovered from
the STPs to depths ranging from 30 to 60 centimeters below ground surface.
While the site does seem to have subsurface deposits of artifacts, the integrity of the site is
uncertain, as the area has been designated as Pits (Pt) by the soil survey, usually a designation
used to denote areas where active mining/gravel operations have taken place. The 2002 Sullivan
City, TX 7.5-minute and the 1956 Garcias, TX 15-minute topographic quadrangles do not
indicate that the area has been used as a gravel pit, but do show areas further to the north and
northeast as designated gravel pits. The site's eligibility for the NRHP cannot be determined
without additional archival and archaeological work.

41HG257 (SamFordyce-P-2)
The site consists of a surface and subsurface scatter of prehistoric and historic material. The site
was originally recorded from a single positive transect STP (T2-STP7), which recovered two
tertiary flakes, three heavily patinated curved aquamarine glass shards, and a ferrous tubular
fragment. An additional nine STPs were excavated during the delineation of the site (D-1 to D9), of which six were positive for cultural material (Table 6-65; see Figure A-113). Cultural
material was recovered to a depth of 50 centimeters below ground surface. The site has been
impacted by the gravel road, which cuts through the center of the site as delineated. The road
itself is down-cut and is 3 feet below the surrounding ground surface. Parallel barbed wire
Cultural Resources Inventory and Impacts Report, Rio Grande City, McAllen, and
Weslaco Stations’ Areas of Responsibility Remote Video Surveillance Upgrade Project

Final
November 2016

fences are located on both sides of the road. Finally, a northeast to southwest trending pipeline
cuts across the southern boundary of the site. Material recovered from shovel tests on the
western side of the road seemed to have more integrity. Material recovered from the east side of
the road had historic material underlying prehistoric material, suggesting that this portion of the
site may be disturbed. Surface visibility during the delineation of the site varied from 90 percent
on the western side of the road to 5 percent on the eastern side of the road, which cuts through
the site. Delineation was largely confined to the survey corridor for the 60-foot access/utility
corridor.

The site is on a relatively flat but slightly southern sloping landform that overlooks the Rio
Grande floodplain to the south. Vegetation noted in the vicinity of the site is dominated by
buffelgrass (Cenchrus ciliare) with scattered shrubs including honey mesquite (Prosopis
glandulosa), huisache (Acacia farnesiana), wild lime (Zanthoxylum fagara), Texas ranger
(Leucophyllum frutescens), blackbrush acacia (Acacia rigidula), spiny hackberry (Celtis pallida),
wild olive (Cordia boissieri), coyotillo (Karwinskia humboldtiana), Texas ebony (Chloroleucon
ebano), and Berlandier wolfberry (Lycium berlandieri) (see Photograph B-398). Examination of
the aerial photographs suggests that the area may have been used for agriculture in the past but is
now reverting to shrubland.

Cultural Resources Inventory and Impacts Report, Rio Grande City, McAllen, and
Weslaco Stations’ Areas of Responsibility Remote Video Surveillance Upgrade Project

Final
November 2016

6-68

Artifacts recovered from the site included prehistoric and historic material. Prehistoric material
was dominated by lithic debitage (one primary flake, two secondary flakes, and five tertiary
flakes of red, tan, and yellow chert). A single stone tool, a white chert multidirectional core tool,
was recovered from the surface (see Photograph B-399). Historic material included heavily
patinated curved glass shards (five aquamarine, two clear glass, and one amber), and one ferrous
tubular fragment. No diagnostic prehistoric or historic material was recorded during the
delineation of the site. As a result, the cultural affiliation of the site is unknown prehistoric and
historic. Given the limited artifact inventory, the site probably represents a limited-use, open
campsite with intrusive historic material. The site boundaries were determined through a
combination of surface expression of artifacts and excavation of STPs. Site delineation was
largely confined to the survey corridor and testing was not extended to the east or west. As a
result, the site boundaries are incomplete and the site may extend further to the west and east. As
currently mapped, the site measures 47 meters north to south by 35 meters east to west and has a
total site area of 1,114 square meters.
While the artifact assemblage is limited and lacks diagnostic material and the eastern portion of
the site has evidence of mixed deposits, the western portion of the site is more intact and includes
subsurface prehistoric material to a depth of 50 centimeters below ground surface. Only the
portion of the site within the current survey corridor was investigated and it is not known
whether portions of the site extend beyond the survey corridor, particularly to the west, where
surface visibility is poor. As a result, site 41HG257 is recommended to have an undetermined
eligibility for the NRHP until the site can be subject to additional archaeological investigations,
including a complete delineation of its boundaries. The portion of the site on the eastern side of
the road lacks integrity with evidence of mixed deposits found to a depth of a depth of 40 cmbgs.
As a result, the portion of the site east of the existing unimproved road retains little integrity and
no additional archaeological investigations are recommended for that portion of the site.
41HG258 (SilverTanks-P-3)
Site 41HG258 was discovered through two positive transect STPs (T1-STP1 and T1-STP2) that
were excavated within the tower footprint, as well as from an associated surface scatter of
artifacts. The site is located on a slight southern slope of a low hill (see Photograph B-400).
Overall surface visibility within the site was good to excellent (30 to 90 percent) with some small
patches of ground cover that provided poor ground visibility (<25 percent). Vegetation at the
site was dominated by buffelgrass (Cenchrus ciliare), with few scattered shrubs (1-3 meters tall)
of honey mesquites (Prosopis glandulosa), Texas paloverde (Parkinsonia texanum), and lead
tree (Leucaena leucocephala).
The boundary of the site was determined through the combination of surface expression of
artifacts, as well as excavation of additional delineation STPs in areas of low surface visibility.
Three additional delineations STPs (D-1 to D-3) were excavated in addition to the three transect
STPs (T1-STP1 to T1-STP3) that were excavated. A total of four positive shovel tests were
excavated within the boundaries of the site, which included two transect and two delineation
STPs (Table 6-66; see Figure A-114). Artifacts from STPs were recorded to a maximum depth
of 40 centimeters below ground surface. A typical shovel test profile from a positive shovel test
excavated at the site consisted of a single stratigraphic level of brown (10YR 5/3) to pale brown
(10YR 6/3) silt loam to loam, which extended to a depth of 50 centimeters below ground surface.
Cultural Resources Inventory and Impacts Report, Rio Grande City, McAllen, and
Weslaco Stations’ Areas of Responsibility Remote Video Surveillance Upgrade Project

Final
November 2016

6-69

Some weakly cemented caliche was noted at the bases of several of the STPs excavated, which
prevented deeper excavation. Site delineation was largely limited to the boundaries of the survey
area itself, but a reconnaissance to the north and northwest of the survey area noted that the site
extends in both directions and may be more concentrated near the top of the low hill to the north
and northwest. As a result, the current site boundary is not considered complete. The site
boundary, as currently defined, measures 85 meters north to south by 59 meters east to west and
has a total site area of 3,261 square meters.

Artifacts recorded at the site include lithic debitage and stone tools. Lithic debitage consisted of
one primary flake, six secondary flakes, 10 tertiary flakes, and seven flakes whose reduction was
not noted. All the lithic debitage was chert. Stone tools recorded at the site include five
unidirectional and multidirectional core tools, one hafted biface (Matamoros type point), and one
medial biface fragment (see Photographs B-401 to B-404). The Matamoros projectile point dates
to a relatively broad range from the Late Archaic to Late Prehistoric periods. The medial biface
fragment could not be identified to a known type. In addition to the artifacts noted above,
thermally altered rock was noted across the site area. While no discrete concentrations of
thermally altered rock were noted during the delineation, the presence of thermally altered rock
across the site suggests that unidentified thermal features may be present. Given the artifact
assemblage, the site probably represents an open campsite and resource processing locus. Given
the presence of diagnostic material, stone tools, thermally altered rock, and subsurface deposits
across the site, the site has potential to have intact deposits that may provide information on
prehistoric adaptations in the area. Additional work, including more extensive subsurface
testing, would be needed to determine whether the site is eligible for the NRHP.

6.2.7 MCS HC Irrigation District #6 Tertiary Option 1
6.2.7.1 Archaeological Resource Investigations
Three STPs (T1-STP1 to T1-STP3) were excavated during the survey of the MC HC Irrigation
District #6 Tertiary Option 1 tower site and associated utility corridor (Table 6-73; see Figure A121). The northern portion of the tower footprint was previously disturbed by a buried pipeline
and the southern portion of the tower footprint had an existing temporary RVSS tower installed
(see Photographs B-440 to B-447). As a result, the STPs were placed between the pipeline
corridor and the temporary tower site. None of the STPs excavated within the tower footprint
were positive for cultural material. Disturbance from the Highway right-of-way and from a
buried gas pipeline prevented the excavation of STPs along the 30-ft utility corridor. One
surface scatter of artifacts was recorded within the utility corridor and extending slightly into the
tower footprint. This resulted in the recording of one archaeological site (41HG259) during the
survey of the MCS HC Irrigation District #6 Tertiary Option 1 tower site and associated utility
corridor.

Cultural Resources Inventory and Impacts Report, Rio Grande City, McAllen, and
Weslaco Stations’ Areas of Responsibility Remote Video Surveillance Upgrade Project

Final
November 2016

6-75

41HG259 (Irrigation-Tert-1-1)
Site 41HG259 was originally recorded from a surface scatter of artifacts within a buried gas
pipeline right-of-way. The buried gas pipeline right-of-way and disturbed right-of-way of
Military Road took up the entire survey corridor, which did not allow for any subsurface testing
within the site boundaries. As a result, the site boundaries were determined by the surface
expression of artifacts alone (see Figure A-122). Site delineation was largely confined to the
survey corridor, though a limited surface reconnaissance was conducted outside of the survey
corridor to determine if the site extended further. To the north, site delineation was limited to the
property fence line, although a very heavy concentration of historic cultural material was noted
within the built-up and disturbed Military Road right-of-way. Additionally, cultural material was
noted outside of the survey corridor and disturbed pipeline right-of-way to the south. Given that
only a limited surface reconnaissance was conducted outside of the survey corridor, the site
boundaries are not considered to be complete (see Photograph B-448). The site, as currently
defined, measures 95 meters east-west by 28 meters north-south and has a total site area of 2,229
square meters.
The site is positioned on the relatively flat floodplain of the Rio Grande River. A large portion
of the site is within a fallow and maintained open field of grass. Heavier vegetation, consisting
predominantly of an overstory of honey mesquite and buffelgrass understory, exists within the
disturbed highway right-of-way between the property fence line and the edge of the highway.
Artifacts recovered from the site consisted of a mix of both prehistoric and historic cultural
material, all of which were recorded from the surface. Prehistoric artifacts consisted of lithic
debitage, stone tools, and thermally altered rock. Lithic debitage consisted of chert and included
seven primary flakes, eight secondary flakes, 11 tertiary flakes, and seven pieces of angular
debris. Stone tools included both hafted and unhafted bifaces, as well as a core tool
(Photographs B-449 to B-458). Hafted bifaces recorded during the delineation of the site
included three possible projectile points and point fragments. One hafted biface (T.1) was thick
and very crudely worked. The blade is oval in form, and the tool has very slight barbs and a
bulbous and rounded stem. While the projectile point resembles the Lone Oak type projectile
point, that type is not common to the area. Given how crudely it is worked, the tool probably
represents a preform. A second hafted biface (T.2) was limited to a proximal fragment of white
chert. The projectile point is triangular in form, with a slightly concave base and evidence of
basal thinning and grinding. The hafted biface probably represents an Early Triangular type,
which is attributed to the Early Archaic period (ca. 3700 to 3600 B.C.). The third hafted biface
(T.4) is elongate triangular in form and made of tan chert. The base is slightly concave and
thinned with vertical flaking. Given its form, the biface is most likely a Kinney Type, which has
been found in association with Middle Archaic occupations. The unhafted biface (T.5) is
circular in form and made of gray chert. The core tool (T.3) is a unidirectional core tool that
represents a turtleback scraper. A single prehistoric feature (F.1), consisting of a light
concentration of 13 fragments of thermally altered rock within a 14-square-meter area, was
recorded during the delineation of the site (see Photograph B-459). The feature may represent a
dispersed hearth.

Cultural Resources Inventory and Impacts Report, Rio Grande City, McAllen, and
Weslaco Stations’ Areas of Responsibility Remote Video Surveillance Upgrade Project

Final
November 2016

6-76

Historic artifacts recorded at the site include ceramic, glass, metal, and brick. Ceramic artifacts
included decorated and 20+ undecorated whiteware (1830 to present), 20+ undecorated ironstone
(1840 to 1930), and undecorated porcelain (1800 to present) (see Photographs B-460 to B-461)
(Florida Museum of Natural History [FLMNH 2016]). Decoration noted on whiteware included
handpainted (1830 to present), annular (1830 to twentieth century), transfer print (black [ca.
1830 to 1850] and light blue [ca. 1826 to 1831]), and fiestaware (FLMNH 2016; Stelle 2011).
Porcelain ceramics included plain body sherds, as well as a porcelain insulator. Glass artifacts
were dominated by curved colorless shards (50+), but also included curved milk (20+) (ca. 1870s
to present), curved olive (10), curved aquamarine (5), curved cobalt blue (5), curved solarized
manganese (2) (ca. 1870s to 1920s), and curved black (dark olive) (1) (ca. 1840s to 1880s) glass
shards (see Photographs B-462 to B-464) (Lockhart 2006; Lindsey 2010a). A single rounded
bottle base fragment was found, which dates ca. 1870s to 1910s (Lindsey 2010b). Metal artifacts
were limited to a single 9mm bullet. Finally, a single brick fragment was noted during the
delineation. Faunal material was limited to unidentifiable bone fragments.
Given the artifact assemblage, the site has at least two occupations, including a prehistoric early
to Middle Archaic occupation that represents an open campsite, as well as a historic farmstead
that extended from the middle 1800s into the twentieth century. The general lack of architectural
material and the heavier concentration of historic artifacts noted in the Military Road right-ofway suggests that the original farmstead might be located further north and may have been
destroyed by the construction of Military Road. While neither the 2016 nor 1963 La Joya, TX
7.5-minute topographic quadrangles note any structures in the vicinity of the site, the 1916
Mission, TX 15-minute topographic quadrangle does show a structure immediately north of
Military Road and the railroad. The portion of the site within the current survey area has been
completely disturbed through the construction of a gas pipeline and Military Road. A surface
reconnaissance outside of the survey corridor noted additional artifacts to the south and the site
extends beyond the survey corridor and disturbance from the buried gas line. As a result, the
eligibility of the site cannot be determined without additional archaeological investigations.
While the site is recommended to have an undetermined eligibility for the NRHP, the portion of
the site within the tower footprint and utility corridor is completely disturbed and not a
contributing element of the site’s potential eligibility for the NRHP.

or eligible for listing in the NRHP are located within the visual APE of the proposed MCS HC

visual APE of the tower site are recreational, utility, and residential buildings.

6.2.8.2 Historic Resource Investigations
One aboveground/architectural historic resource, identified by GSRC personnel, was recorded
within the visual APE of the proposed MCS Penitas Pump Alternate tower site. Penitas
Cemetery (aka San Antonio Cemetery) is unnumbered by THC (Photograph B-473) is located
0.32 km (0.20 mile) northwest from the center of the tower site. The cemetery was established
circa 1850. It is approximately 1.24 acres and is surrounded by a 3-foot-tall chain-link fence. A
wrought-iron gate and stucco-clad arch form the main entryway and sit on the west (primary)
side of the cemetery. A flagpole sits inside the front gate to the right. The tower site is likely to
be visible from all points in the cemetery but does not constitute as an adverse visual elements.
The existing structures present in the visual APE of the tower site consist of small residential
buildings.

One aboveground/architectural historic resource, identified by GSRC personnel, was recorded

6.2.13.2 Historic Resource Investigations
One non-historic aboveground resource identified by GSRC personnel, was recorded within the
visual APE of the proposed MCS Inspiration Canal Alternate tower site. The Rio Grande Valley
State Veterans Cemetery is unnumbered by THC and is located 0.58 km (0.36 mile) south from
the center of the tower site. The cemetery was established in 2006. It is approximately 25 acres
and is surrounded by grass lawns. The front entrance to the cemetery has a stucco clad arch with
signage that reads RIO GRANDE VALLEY STATE VETERANS CEMETERY and sits on the
east (primary) side of the cemetery. The tower site is likely to be visible all points in the
cemetery but does not constitute as an adverse visual element, as there are existing towers and
new construction within the area already. Additional structures present in the visual APE of the
tower site consist of small residential and commercial buildings, as well as farmland.
6.2.14 MCS Madero Primary
6.2.14.1 Archaeological Resource Investigations

was within an existing citrus grove, and ground visibility across the tower site and northern
portion of the access and utility corridor was excellent (95 to 100 percent). One of the STPs was
positive for archaeological material, which resulted in the recording of one IO (IO-Madero-P-1)
during the survey of the MCS Madero Primary tower site and its associated access and utility
corridor (Table 6-80; see Figure A-129).

IO-Madero-P-1 (Madero-P-1)
IO-Madero-P-1 was originally recorded from a single positive STP, T2-STP3. A single
secondary chert flake was recovered from the STP. An additional five STPs (D-1 to D-5) were
excavated to further delineate the find and to assess if any additional sub-surface cultural
material was present (see Figure A-130). None of the additional excavated STPs were positive
for cultural material (Table 6-81). An extensive surface examination of the area identified one
additional artifact, a flake of chert. Given the limited amount of material found the locus is
considered to be an IO (IO-Madero-P-1) and not an archaeological site. IO-Madero-P-1 by its
nature is not considered significant and is not considered to be eligible for inclusion in the
NRHP.

6-83

6.2.14.2 Historic Resource Investigations
The NRHP-listed La Lomita Historic District (Reference No. 75002165) is located within the
visual APE of the proposed MCS Madero Primary tower site. The District is approximately 3.45
acres in total and is located approximately 5 miles south of Mission, Texas, on the south side of
FM 1016, on the hill above the Rio Grande River. Inside the boundaries of the historic district,
there is one architecturally significant structure, built in 1899 by the Oblates of Mary Immaculate
as a chapel meant to serve the ranch workers at Rancho La Lomita. The City of Mission, Texas,
was named for the La Lomita Mission. The Mission became a RTHL in 1964, and was listed in
the National Register in 1975 under the areas of local significance in transportation, exploration
and settlement, architecture, and religion, with periods of significance from 1875-1924. The
Mission itself (see Photograph B-523) is located 0.25 km (0.16 mile) northwest from the center
of the tower site. The tower site is likely to be visible from all points in the District but does not
constitute an adverse visual element, as there are existing towers within the area already, as well
as new construction. Other existing structures present in the visual APE of the tower site are a
combination of large and small commercial and residential buildings, and the Anzalduas
recreational park to the north and west of the District.
One OTHM, identified by GSRC personnel, is recorded within the visual APE of the proposed
MCS Madero Primary tower site. The OTHM is the La Lomita Chapel marker (Marker No.
2997) (see Photograph B-527). The marker is a medallion, with two plaques beneath. One
plaque is for the state listing, and the other plaque is for the National Register listing. The
proposed MCS Madero Primary tower site is visible from the marker and, while it does
constitute an adverse visual element, the resource would not be directly impacted because the use
of adjacent properties has changed dramatically, as well as already present adverse visual
elements. As a result, no adverse effects on architectural or aboveground resources that are
eligible for the NRHP are anticipated.

6.2.20.2 Historic Resource Investigations
The boundary of the Louisiana – Rio Grande Canal Company Irrigation System NRHP-listed
historic district (Reference No. 95001284) is located within the visual APE of the proposed MCS
Hidalgo POE Alternate tower site. The historic district contains approximately 3,135 acres and
runs from the Rio Grande in the south, north along Bridge Street (which turns into Depot Street
and North 23rd Street), east/west just south of Freddy Gonzalez Drive in Edinburgh, returns
south at County Road 5959C and Border Road, and stops just south of Military Highway at the
southern border. The boundaries of the historic district inside the APE include approximately 11
blocks of the historic district and include two groups of architecturally significant structures, the
Old Hidalgo Courthouse and Buildings (NRHP No. 80004136) by Juan Rios, and the First-Lift
Pumphouse (Reference No. 95001284).
The Old Hidalgo Courthouse and Buildings (see Photograph B-569) are located at Flora and 1st
Streets. They were constructed circa 1875 by Juan Rios, and the areas of significance include
politics/government, exploration/settlement, architecture, and commerce, with a period of
significance of 1875-1899. Additionally, the Old Hidalgo Pumphouse Museum and World
Birding Center (National Register No. 80004136) lies within the APE (see Photograph B-570).
The lower Rio Grande Valley was only suitable for a cattle grazing up until steam-powered
irrigation was introduced to the area in the early 1900s. After the establishment of the irrigation
plant, approximately 40,000 acres of land was able to be irrigated, which contributed to the
expansion of Pharr/San Juan/Alamo areas.
The proposed MCS Hidalgo POE Alternate tower site is visible from this historic district, and
while it does constitute an adverse visual element, the resources would not be directly impacted,
as adverse visual elements already exist, such as a new water tower and existing communications
towers. As a result, no adverse effects on architectural or aboveground resources that are eligible
for the NRHP are anticipated. Other structures present are a combination of large and small
commercial and residential buildings, communications towers, a water tower, and new municipal
buildings.
One OTHM, identified by GSRC personnel, is recorded within the visual APE of the proposed
MCS Hidalgo POE Alternative tower site. The OTHM is the Old Hidalgo Pumphouse Museum
and World Birding Center marker (Marker No. 2408). The marker is a medallion with a plaque.
The proposed MCS Hidalgo POE Alternate tower site is visible from this historic marker and the
resource would not be directly impacted, as adverse visual elements exist, such as a new water
tower, existing communications towers, and new construction. As a result, no adverse effects on
architectural or aboveground resources that are eligible for the NRHP are anticipated. Other
structures present within the visual APE of the tower site are a combination of large and small
commercial and residential buildings, communications towers, a water tower, and new municipal
buildings.

6.3.4 WSL Pharr POE South Tertiary Option 1
6.3.4.1 Archaeological Resource Investigations
The area encompassing the proposed WSL Pharr POE South Tertiary Option 1 tower site and its
associated access road was previously surveyed by two investigations (491 and 500) that overlap
completely with the proposed tower footprint and access road. In addition, two archaeological
sites recorded during those surveys (41HG162 and 41HG163) have been evaluated for inclusion
in the NRHP and have been determined to be ineligible. Aerial photographs note that the area
has been completely cleared and leveled as recently as 2014. Currently there is a temporary
tower site established on the site (see Photographs B-595 to B-602). As a result, no subsurface
testing was conducted at the tower site or along the access road, but both areas were subject to
pedestrian survey.

6.3.4.2 Historic Resource Investigations
The proposed WSL Pharr POE South Tertiary Option 1 tower site is within the boundary of the
Louisiana – Rio Grande Canal Company Irrigation System NRHP-listed historic district
(Reference No. 95001284). The historic district contains approximately 3,135 acres and runs
from the Rio Grande in the south, north along Bridge Street (which turns into Depot Street and
North 23rd Street), and east/west just south of Freddy Gonzalez Drive in Edinburgh, and returns
south at County Road 5959C and Border Road, and stops just south of Military Road at the
southern border.
The proposed Pharr POE Tertiary Option 1 tower site is visible from this historic district, and
while it does constitute an adverse visual element, the resources would not be directly impacted,
as already present adverse visual elements exist, such as a new water tower and existing
communications towers. As a result, no adverse effects on architectural or aboveground
resources that are eligible for the NRHP are anticipated. Other structures present are a
combination of large and small commercial and residential buildings, communications towers, a
water tower, and new municipal buildings. The existing structures visible within the visual APE
of the tower site are small utility buildings.
One aboveground/architectural historic resource, identified by GSRC personnel, was recorded
within the visual APE of the proposed WSL Pharr POE South POE Tertiary Option 1 tower site.
An unnamed and unnumbered cemetery (see Photograph B-603) is located 0.57 km (0.36 mile)
northeast from the center of the tower site. The date of the establishment of the cemetery is
unknown. The cemetery is located off Pharr International Bridge Road, and south of Doffin
Canal Road, in the middle of farmland. It is approximately 0.39 acre and is surrounded by a 3foot-tall chain-link fence. A chain-link gate sits on the north (primary) side of the cemetery.
The tower site is likely to be visible from all points in the cemetery but does not constitute an
adverse visual element, as there are existing towers within the area already. Additional
structures present in the APE consist of small utility buildings, as well as farmland.

6.3.14 WSL Retamal South Primary
6.3.14.1 Archaeological Resource Investigations
Three STPs (T1-STP1 to T1-STP3) were excavated within the tower footprint during the survey
of the MCS Retamal South Primary tower site (see Photographs B-673 to B-678). The existing
road and paralleling water irrigation lines on both sides of the 30-ft access corridor prevented
excavation of STPs along that portion. The 60-foot access and utility corridor was entirely
within the footprint of an approximately 10-foot-high levee road, which also prevented the
excavation of STPs. Both these sections were subject to pedestrian survey. None of the STPs
were positive for archaeological material and no archaeological resources were recorded during
the survey of the WSL Donna Canal Alternate tower site and its associated access and utility
corridor (Table 6-100; see Figure A-149). The previously recorded archaeological site
(41HG35), which overlaps with a portion of the 60-foot access and utility corridor were not
relocated during the current survey. This portion of the survey corridor is on top of an
approximately 10-ft-high levee, which encompasses the entire survey corridor. It is likely that
the site has been destroyed by the construction of the levee.

6.3.15 WSL Retamal South Alternate
6.3.15.1 Archaeological Resource Investigations
The area encompassing the tower footprint of the MCS Retamal South Alternate tower site has
been heavily disturbed with pushpiles evident along the edges of the tower footprint and a deep
irrigation ditch and buried irrigation pipeline extending across a portion of the tower site (see
Photographs B-679 to B-686). Given the heavy disturbance and presence of a buried pipeline,
STPs were not excavated at the tower site. The survey corridor of the 30-foot access road was
entirely encompassed by an existing unimproved road and associated drainage ditches which also
prevented the excavation of STPs along the access road. The tower footprint and access road
had good to excellent surface visibility (80 to 90 percent) and were subject to pedestrian survey.
No archaeological resources were recorded during the survey of the WSL Retamal South
Alternate tower site and its associated access corridor.

6.3.17 WSL Whiskey Tree Alternate
6.3.17.1 Archaeological Resource Investigations
Three STPs (T1-STP1 to T1-STP3) were excavated within the tower footprint and three STPs
(T2-STP1 to T2-STP3) were excavated along the access road during the survey of the MCS
Whiskey Tree Alternate tower site (see Photographs B-695 to B-701). Two of the STPs (T2STP2 and T2-STP3) excavated along the access road encountered a buried white plastic sheet at
10 cmbs, which indicated that an unmarked buried utility or irrigation line was present along
both side of the access road. Given the presence of a buried line, no additional STPs were
excavated along the original access corridor and that portion of the road was subject to
pedestrian survey. After the original access road was surveyed a new realigned access road was
developed. The realigned access road followed an existing unimproved road which
encompassed the whole 30-ft corridor. As a result, excavation of STPs along the realigned
access road was not possible and the road was subject to pedestrian survey. None of the STPs
were positive for archaeological material and no archaeological resources were recorded during
the survey of the WSL Whiskey Tree Alternate tower site and its associated access and utility
corridors (Table 6-102; see Figure A-151).

6.3.17.2 Historic Resource Investigations
One aboveground/architectural historic resource, identified by GSRC personnel, was recorded
within the visual APE of the proposed WSL Whiskey Tree Alternate tower site. Guzman-Toluca
Cemetery (Cemetery ID No. HG-C005) (see Photograph B-702) is located 0.57 km (0.36 mile)
northeast from the center of the tower site. The cemetery was established circa 1904. It is
approximately 0.30 acre and is surrounded by a 3-foot-tall chain-link fence. A chain-link gate
sits on the west (primary) side of the cemetery. The tower site is likely to be visible from all
points in the cemetery, but does not constitute an adverse visual element, as there are existing
towers and new construction within the area already. The additional structures present within the
visual APE of the tower consist of grain silos, commercial buildings, and farmland.
Cultural Resources Inventory and Impacts Report, Rio Grande City, McAllen, and
Weslaco Stations’ Areas of Responsibility Remote Video Surveillance Upgrade Project

Final
November 2016

SECTION 7.0
SUMMARY AND RECOMMENDATIONS

7-1

7.0

SUMMARY AND RECOMMENDATIONS

GSRC personnel conducted intensive pedestrian archaeological surveys at 68 proposed primary,
alternate, tertiary option, and relay tower location sites for the Rio Grande Valley RVSS Upgrade
project. Surveys conducted included both pedestrian survey and excavation of STPs across the
tower sites as well their associated access and utility corridors. A total of 28 archaeological
resources were recorded during the surveys. Another two previously recorded archaeological
resources were revisited and updated during the archaeological survey conducted for this project.
Table 7-1 summarizes the eligibility recommendations for the new and updated archaeological
resources identified during this survey. Eleven of the 28 archaeological sites recorded are
recommended Not Eligible for listing in the NRHP. No additional archaeological investigations
are recommended for these archaeological resources. The remaining 17 archaeological resources
would require additional testing to determine their eligibility for the NRHP and are
recommended to have an undetermined eligibility until additional testing can be done to
determine if they are indeed eligible for the NRHP. Only a limited portion of those sites have
been recorded during the current survey due to the limits of the survey corridor.

41SR392

41SR403
Undetermined
determine the extent and integrity of the newly
recorded portions of the site.

Two previously recorded archaeological sites were also revisited and updated. Site 41SR395
consisted of a previously recorded prehistoric site. The limited STPs excavated at the site during
the current survey confirmed the presence of subsurface deposits and a surface reconnaissance of
the site noted that its condition has changed little since it was initially recorded. As a result,
GSRC personnel concur with the recommendations of the original recorders that the site is
eligible for the NRHP. The second previously recorded site, 41SR403, consists of a prehistoric
site that is interpreted to be a prehistoric quarry and resource procurement site. The original
recorders recommended the site as not eligible for listing in the NRHP. During the current
survey an extension of the site was recorded, which contained stone tools and possible
subsurface deposits. The surface scatter of artifacts extended beyond the current survey
boundaries to both the north and south. Given the extent of the newly recorded portion of the
site and the fact that the site could not be fully documented due to the limits of the survey
corridor, GSRC recommends that the site have an undetermined eligibility until additional
investigations can be done and the boundaries of the site can be more fully defined.
The aboveground/architectural historic resource survey revisited previously recorded NRHPlisted and NRHP-eligible historic resources, OTHMs including RTHLs, and cemeteries that were
located within the 0.5-mile visual APE of each of the proposed tower sites. One new
aboveground/architectural historic resource was noted during the historic resource surveys,
which consisted of a newly recorded cemetery (Chapeno-2).
Table 7-2 presents the recommended effects determination for each of the proposed tower sites
surveyed during the current investigation. The effects determination assumes that the entire
proposed tower site and its associated access and utility corridors as mapped would be disturbed.
For the purpose of the effects determination, sites with an undetermined eligibility that would be
affected by construction are considered to have an adverse effect unless NRHP eligibility testing
and data recovery mitigation measures, if necessary, are implemented. Only
aboveground/architectural historic resources that are listed or are eligible for listing in the NRHP
were considered in determining the effects determinations for each of the proposed tower sites.
